        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.8b08380"><meta property="og:title" content="Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K+ Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/acsami.8b08380.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Heteroatom-doped carbon is an attractive material for anodes in lithium-/sodium-ion batteries as a replacement for traditional graphite anodes. However, the complex fabrication process and high cost limit practical applications of these carbon materials. Here, we report a low-cost, natural potassium-doped carbon material, which is directly carbonized from the coconut endocarp—a kind of high potassium-containing biomass material. The obtained carbon structure features an expanded d(002)-spacing (0.4 nm) originating from the superhigh potassium content (6654 mg kg–1). Because of the improvement on charge transfer kinetics and electrical properties, the potassium-doped carbon anode exhibits promising electrochemical performance in sodium-ion batteries, including high initial reversible capacity (314 mAh g–1) and good cycle stability (289 mAh g–1 after 200 cycles). Additionally, this work opens up a new approach for the design of heteroatom-doped carbon materials from the viewpoint of being naturally environmental friendly."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/acsami.8b08380.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '1dd6f492-c46c-4e8d-8c64-88b1a13a1eee'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Expanding Interlayer Spacing of Hard Carbon by Natural K&lt;sup&gt;+&lt;/sup&gt; Doping to Boost Na-Ion Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.8b08380", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D27030%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DFeng%2BWu%252C%2BLu%2BLiu%252C%2BYifei%2BYuan%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D32%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b08380%26title%3DExpanding%2BInterlayer%2BSpacing%2Bof%2BHard%2BCarbon%2Bby%2BNatural%2BK%252B%2BDoping%2Bto%2BBoost%2BNa-Ion%2BStorage%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D27038%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2018" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/medium/am-2018-083809_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08380&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Heteroatom-doped carbon is an attractive material for anodes in lithium-/sodium-ion batteries as a replacement for traditional graphite anodes. However, the complex fabrication process and high cost limit practical applications of these carbon materials. Here, we report a low-cost, natural potassium-doped carbon material, which is directly carbonized from the coconut endocarp—a kind of high potassium-containing biomass material. The obtained carbon structure features an expanded <i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub>-spacing (0.4 nm) originating from the superhigh potassium content (6654 mg kg<sup>–1</sup>). Because of the improvement on charge transfer kinetics and electrical properties, the potassium-doped carbon anode exhibits promising electrochemical performance in sodium-ion batteries, including high initial reversible capacity (314 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and good cycle stability (289 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles). Additionally, this work opens up a new approach for the design of heteroatom-doped carbon materials from the viewpoint of being naturally environmental friendly.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D27030%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DFeng%2BWu%252C%2BLu%2BLiu%252C%2BYifei%2BYuan%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D32%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b08380%26title%3DExpanding%2BInterlayer%2BSpacing%2Bof%2BHard%2BCarbon%2Bby%2BNatural%2BK%252B%2BDoping%2Bto%2BBoost%2BNa-Ion%2BStorage%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D27038%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292006&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Potassium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Potassium" class="article__tags__link">Potassium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon">hard carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Natural+Doping&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_natural%20doping">natural doping</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Interlayer+Spacing&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_interlayer%20spacing">interlayer spacing</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Commercial lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have played a significant role in portable electronic devices and electric vehicles because of their outstanding cycling performance and high energy density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> However, their practical application presents inevitable issues in terms of scarce resources, uneven distribution, and gradually rising prices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 4 to 7">(4−7)</a> Sodium and lithium have similar physical and chemical properties that largely determine the performance of a battery. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 8 to 12">(8−12)</a> In addition, on the basis of the ubiquitous distribution of sodium resources and its low cost, recently, investigators have given much attention to sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) as a potential commercial alternative to LIBs for satisfying enormous energy requirements of the society. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Because the sodium ion has a larger ionic radius compared to that of lithium, it is of utmost importance to manufacture a type of appropriate host material that must possess an adequately large interlayer structure for Na<sup>+</sup> to shuttle freely. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> The recommended anode materials include carbon materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 18 to 22">(18−22)</a> compound materials (metal oxides/sulfides, phosphorus, and titanates), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 23 to 26">(23−26)</a> and alloys (Ge, Sn with Na). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> In particular, carbon materials have received great interest because of their low cost, abundance, and good conductivity. However, most importantly, well-designed structures of carbon are required to facilitate the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and accelerate solid-state diffusion kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> For instance, sufficient layer spacing, defect sites, and pores are critical to providing abundant active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ionic storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> To date, exploring high-performance carbon anodes with flexible, appropriate structures and excellent electrochemical performance at a reasonable price is still a challenge for the broad utilization of SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Hard carbon has been investigated as the potential anode material since the development of SIBs at its early stages. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> To obtain optimized nanostructures with low cost, amorphous hard carbon derived from biomass with a large <i>d</i><sub>002</sub>-spacing and disordered sections has been recently attracting much research interest. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 32 to 35">(32−35)</a> This method has been applied to several renewable biomass materials such as banana peel, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> peanut shells, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> pomelo peel, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> and corn cobs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Yet, these reported methods require the addition of an extra step, namely, solvent pretreatment, to introduce the ionic species that are necessary to support the increased <i>d</i><sub>002</sub>-spacing in the later carbonization stage, which inevitably introduces high cost for material processing.</div><div class="NLM_p">Here, we choose coconut endocarp, which is originally rich in K<sup>+</sup> in its natural form of existence, as the precursor to prepare hard carbon using a simple carbonization synthesis route without any time- and money-consuming solvent pretreatment (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>). The morphology, structure, and electrochemical performance of hard carbon prepared under different temperatures were investigated. Interestingly, the heteroatoms of potassium (K) in the hard carbon material are confirmed to improve the electrochemical performance of batteries. The K<sup>+</sup> doped into the hard carbon could effectively expand its interlayer spacing due to the larger radius of K<sup>+</sup> (0.138 nm). The low price, facile and environmentally friendly synthesis process, and good electrochemical performance are attractive factors that endow this material with strong potential as a successful anode in SIBs.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/medium/am-2018-083809_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0003.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Diagram of the Synthetic Route for Natural K-Doped Hard Carbon Anode</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08380&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.1.  Material Fabrication</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical procedure, the fresh coconut endocarp was cleaned with distilled water several times and dried at 200 °C for 2 h under an argon gas flow. The dried materials were immediately heated to 900, 1000, 1100, and 1200 °C under an argon atmosphere for 2 h inside a tube furnace to obtain the final carbon materials. Hereafter, we refer to these carbon materials obtained at different temperatures with the acronyms HC-900 (hard carbon at 900 °C), HC-1000 (hard carbon at 1000 °C), HC-1100 (hard carbon at 1100 °C), and HC-1200 (hard carbon at 1200 °C). After the carbonization process, the obtained HC-1100 sample was leached in a vial with 0.5 M HCl for 12 h to remove the inorganic ions from its bulk. The acid-washed sample was rinsed with deionized water three times and then dried at 120 °C overnight under vacuum. We refer to this particular sample as the HC-1100H.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed on an SII TG 6200 thermoanalyzer under an argon gas flow at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were recorded on a D/MAX-RB X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation with an X-ray wavelength of 1.5418 Å. The crystalline structural parameters were calculated using the Bragg equation. Raman spectra were performed on Renishaw micro-Raman spectroscopy equipped with an InVia-Reflex confocal microprobe. Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) measurements were carried out at the 12-ID-C beamline at the Advanced Photon Source of Argonne National Laboratory. The sample-to-detector distance was about 2 m. A charge-coupled device detector was used to acquire images with typical exposure times in the range of 0.1–1.0 s for a single measurement. The samples were placed in quartz capillaries for all of the measurements. X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS) were recorded on an ESCALAB 250 surface analysis system. Field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) was conducted on a Zeiss Supra 55/3195 microscope. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was conducted on a JEOL JEM-ARM200CF microscope. The mesoporous structure was characterized by N<sub>2</sub> sorption measurement at 77 K using an ASAP 2020 HD88 Quantachrome instrument. The elemental composition was recorded on an ICP-OES Optima 8300 inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometer.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Hard carbon electrodes were prepared by pasting a mixture of active materials, carbon black, and poly(vinylidene fluoride) at a weight ratio of 8:1:1 onto a copper foil and then dried at 80 °C for 12 h under vacuum. The average mass loading density of the active anode material in each electrode slice was 0.12 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The 2032-type coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox using sodium foil as the counter electrode and glass fiber separator absorbing electrolyte (1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in 1 L of propylene carbonate (PC) with (5 vol %) fluoroethylene carbonate). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were carried out at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> on a CHI660E electrochemical workstation. Galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were carried out at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0 V using a Land CT2001A battery tester.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The TGA (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a) reveals the thermal decomposition behavior of the fresh coconut endocarp under argon flow. The raw materials first underwent a dehydration process between 30 and 100 °C and then became soft in the range of 100–250 °C; after that, the materials started to pyrolyze and carbonize until 800 °C. On the basis of this result, we thus designed a rational synthetic process for the fabrication of biomass-derived carbon materials, as described in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="Section 2">Section </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="2">2</a>. The crystal structure of hard carbon materials was characterized by XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). All of the samples show two broad diffraction peaks at about 23.0 and 43.7°, which are assigned to the (002) and (101) planes of hard carbon, respectively. The broad peaks suggest that the hard carbon materials have a disordered or amorphous bulk structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37−39)</a> Note that the (002) peak slightly shifts to the smaller 2θ on increasing the carbonation temperature to 1100 °C, corresponding to a gradual expanding trend of the <i>d</i>-spacing. <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a> exhibits the calculated <i>d</i>-spacing based on the XRD patterns using the Bragg equation. The <i>d</i>-spacing ranged from 0.387 to 0.401 nm, which was larger than the theoretical minimum value of <i>d</i>-spacing (0.370 nm) for Na-ion insertion. Therefore, the expanding <i>d</i>-spacing was considered to be more favorable for Na-ion diffusion and storage in such hard carbon materials. The Raman spectrum can reflect the crystallinity of carbon materials. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c, the Raman spectra show a broad D-band and a relatively sharp G-band at 1334 and 1568 cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Generally, the D-band is related to the disordered degree of carbon, whereas the G-band corresponds to graphitic carbon. In such carbon materials, the intensity ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is about 1.15, which suggests their highly disordered structures due to voids and defects. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref40" aria-label="reference 36 and 40">(36,40)</a> For the use of carbon materials as anodes in Na-ion batteries, both the enlarged <i>d</i>-spacing and disordered structure were considered to be beneficial for the diffusion and storage of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Such hard carbon materials thus may exhibit good electrochemical performance in Na-ion batteries.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/medium/am-2018-083809_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0004.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Thermogravimetric profile of the fresh coconut endocarp. (b) XRD patterns and (c) Raman spectra of hard carbon materials. (d) Initial voltage profiles and (e) long cycling performance of hard carbon materials at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08380&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Structural Properties of Hard Carbon Materials</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" (deg)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>d</i>-spacing (nm)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub></th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">ICE (%)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-900</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">900</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">22.94</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.3874</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.14</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">61.2</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-1000</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1000</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">22.26</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.4010</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.19</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">68.5</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-1100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">22.14</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.4012</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.15</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">69.2</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-1200</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1200</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">22.90</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.3880</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.31</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">57.9</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-1100H</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">22.54</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.3941</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.18</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">65.2</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of the above-mentioned structural properties, we evaluated their electrochemical performance in Na-ion cells by galvanostatic discharge–charge testing. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d exhibits their initial voltage profiles. Among the four samples, the HC-1100 sample delivers the highest charge capacity of 313.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with the highest initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 69.2%. Notably, it also shows the lowest discharge and charge plateaus, which are considered to be attributed to the largest <i>d</i>-spacing of the (002) plane. The enlarged <i>d</i>-spacing of carbon materials has been reported to induce a lower potential for the intercalation or extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> The irreversible capacity of the first cycle can mainly be ascribed to the electrolyte decomposition and sequent formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the electrode surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e shows the long cycling performance of these materials at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. As expected, the HC-1100 has the best performance among these four samples, including the highest reversible capacity and the most stable cycling performance. It can maintain a high capacity of 288.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles, with a high capacity retention of 92.1%.</div><div class="NLM_p">To understand the mechanism why the HC-1100 has the best electrochemical performance, we carried out more and deeper characterizations to analyze its structural feature and chemical composition. The total element analysis in the HC-1100 was investigated by inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP-MS), and it reveals a high potassium (K) concentration in the carbon material bulk. Therefore, we designed a comparative test, where we washed the HC-1100 with 0.5 M HCl solution to remove some K<sup>+</sup> ions and then dried it to obtain a new sample, HC-1100H. The ICP-MS results show that the K<sup>+</sup> content of these two samples is 6654 mg kg<sup>–1</sup> (HC-1100) and 2967 mg kg<sup>–1</sup> (HC-1100H). Obviously, the K<sup>+</sup> content dramatically decreased after the acid-leaching. The XRD patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a) show that the (002) peak slightly shifts toward the larger 2θ after the acid-leaching, suggesting that the <i>d</i>-spacing becomes smaller because K<sup>+</sup> partially leached out from the lattice spacing of carbon materials. This finding confirms that the high K<sup>+</sup> content existing in the carbon materials expands the <i>d</i>-spacing of graphitic layers.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/medium/am-2018-083809_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0005.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns, (b) 77 K N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms, and (c) pore size distributions of the HC-1100 and HC-1100H samples. (d, e) Experimental data (open square) and fitting curve (red line) of SAXS data for the HC-1100 (d) and HC-1100H (e) samples. (f) Micropore size distributions in the HC-1100 and HC-1100H samples obtained from the fitting curves of SAXS data.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08380&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Both N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption and SAXS measurements were carried out to investigate the microstructure of these two samples. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b,c shows the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms and the mesopore size distributions, respectively. The high specific surface areas of 78.97 and 103.83 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> with pore volumes of 0.03 and 0.04 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> were measured for the HC-1100 and HC-1100H samples, respectively. In general, a relatively lower specific surface can reduce the contact area that decreases the SEI formation, thus leading to a higher initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 46 to 48">(46−48)</a> In addition, compared to the HC-1100, the HC-1100H displays a higher pore volume with a bigger mesoporous distribution in the range of 3–5 nm, which were the nanovoids caused by the acid-leaching. The micropores of samples were further analyzed by SAXS tests. The SAXS curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d,e) show an obvious difference at the <i>q</i> range from 0.09 to 0.9 Å that was fitted using Irena Software. This difference indicates that the micropores changed as the carbon material was etched using HCl. For simplicity, the micropores were approximated with spherical shape, and the pore sizes were assumed to follow the Schulz–Zimm function. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f exhibits their pore size distributions based on the fit curves. The mean sizes of micropores of the HC-1100 and HC-1100H samples were 0.9 and 1.0 nm, respectively. The results indicate that the pore size of carbon materials increased after the acid-leaching.</div><div class="NLM_p">The XPS results confirm their elemental compositions. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> shows the C 1s XPS spectra of these two samples. For the HC-1100 sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a), the fit of the C 1s spectrum exhibits three divided peaks—a main peak at 284.5 eV is related to the graphitic-sp<sup>2</sup> carbon, whereas two other peaks at 285.6 and 287.8 eV correspond to the sp<sup>3</sup> carbon and O–C═O bonds, respectively. Generally, the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon means that C atoms are arranged in conjugated honeycomb lattices, and the sp<sup>3</sup> carbon can be related to the defects on graphite layers caused by the chemical substitution or physical destruction. Comparatively, the HC-1100 sample has a high chemical content of sp<sup>2</sup> carbon, indicating its stable graphite structure. After the acid-leaching, the contents of sp<sup>3</sup> carbon and O–C═O bonds decreased, whereas the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon greatly increased. This finding suggests that some of the graphite structure has been destroyed and the carbon composition has changed to a certain degree.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/medium/am-2018-083809_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. C 1s XPS spectra of the HC-1100 (a) and HC-1100H (b) samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08380&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The microstructure and morphology of these two samples were further investigated by SEM and TEM measurements, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. The SEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a,b) illustrate the bulk morphologies where the HC-1100H particles become smaller after acid-leaching. It is possibly caused by the cracking of hard carbon due to the removal of K<sup>+</sup> from the bulk structure. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,e shows the TEM morphologies of two samples. It can be observed from low-mag TEM images that both of them exhibit a flakelike morphology with a size of hundreds of nanometers, and apparently, the acid-leaching treatment hardly changes the overall morphology. The high-resolution (HR)-TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d) of the HC-1100 demonstrates the graphitic structure with the <i>d</i>-spacing generally ranging from 0.41 to 0.43 nm. After the acid-leaching (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f), the <i>d</i>-spacing of the graphitic carbon decreases to around 0.395 nm, which also implies the successful removal of K<sup>+</sup> ions from the interlayers of graphitic carbon during the acid-leaching treatment.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/medium/am-2018-083809_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a, b) SEM images of the HC-1100 (a) and HC-1100H (b) samples. (c–f) TEM images of the HC-1100 (c, d) and HC-1100H (e, f) samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08380&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Their electrochemical behavior was evaluated by CV and galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements using 2032-type coin cells. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b shows the typical cyclic voltammetry of the HC-1100 and HC-1100H electrodes at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0 V. Both of them show two irreversible cathodic peaks at ∼1.25 and 0.6 V during the first reduction process, corresponding to the reductive decomposition of the electrolyte and the subsequent formation of SEI layers on the electrode surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Another sharp cathodic peak at ∼0.05 V is attributed to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into hard carbon materials, whereas in the following oxidation process, an anodic peak at ∼0.2 V represents the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from carbon layers. Note that the cathodic peaks at 1.25 and 0.6 V almost disappear in the subsequent cycles, indicating that the SEI layers are mainly formed during the first reduction process and can remain stable during the next cycles. Comparatively, the HC-1100H electrode shows a higher irreversible area between the first and second reduction processes than that of the HC-1100 electrode. This finding can be ascribed to the larger surface area of the HC-1100H sample caused by the acid-leaching. That is to say, the HC-1100 materials may deliver higher reversible capacity because of their stable structure.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/medium/am-2018-083809_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a, b) Cyclic voltammograms of the HC-1100 (a) and HC-1100H (b) samples. (c, d) Charge–discharge profiles of the HC-1100 (a) and HC-1100H (b) samples. (e, f) Capacity of sloping/plateau above and below 0.1 V for the initial three cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08380&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,d exhibits their voltage profiles of the prime three cycles at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0 V. Both of them show a typical intercalation/extraction electrochemical characteristic of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into/from carbon anodes. The HC-1100 electrode delivers a high initial discharge capacity of 453.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, with the ICE of 69.2%. However, the HC-1100H electrode shows a relatively low discharge capacity of 378.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, with the ICE of 65.2%. During the initial discharge process, a high discharge potential between 1.3 and 0.6 V can be attributed to the SEI formation. For the second and third cycles, a slope plateau is mainly attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup>-ion insertion/extraction into/from nanopores or defects of the disorder carbon matrix, whereas a much lower plateau at ∼0.05 V corresponds to the Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion/extraction between graphitic lattices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 50 to 52">(50−52)</a> These two plateaus are in good agreement with the two pairs of redox peaks in cyclic voltammograms. From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e,f, we can find that the capacities at the 0.05 V plateau and during the sloping plateau dramatically decrease after the first cycle, but they can remain stable after the second cycle. In addition, the HC-1100 sample shows a higher percentage of 0.05 V plateau capacity in the whole capacity than that of the HC-1100H sample, suggesting that the Na storage capacity mainly depends on the insertion capability into the graphitic lattices. This indicates that the more and larger <i>d</i>-spacings are beneficial for sodium-ion diffusion and storage.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> illustrates their long cycling performance and rate performance. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a, the HC-1100 electrode delivers a high initial charge capacity of 313.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, with the ICE of 69.2%, whereas the corresponding values of the HC-1100H electrode are 246.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 65.2%. Note that the ICE of the HC-1100 is higher than that of the HC-1100H by 4%, which was attributed to the more stable structure of the HC-1100. During the next 10 cycles, the CE of both the electrodes gradually increased to ∼99.5%. This process represents the activation of active materials. From the viewpoint of long cycling, the HC-1100 shows much higher reversible capacities and better cycling stability than does the HC-1100H. After 200 cycles, the HC-1100 retains a high capacity of 288.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Comparatively, the HC-1100H shows a normal cycling for 100 cycles, but it is no longer stable for the next 100 cycles. One possible cause of the failure is the degradation of PC electrolyte that may became unstable and decompose on nanopores or defects under long-term electrochemical reactions. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b shows a comparison of their rate performance. Generally, the capacity retention of both the samples decreases on increasing the current rate. The HC-1100 electrode has a better rate performance than that of the HC-1100H electrode because the HC-1100 shows a much higher capacity and better stability on cycling at each current rate. Notably, for the HC-1100 electrode, the capacity can recover to 329.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density is returned to its initial value of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. These results reveal that the HC-1100 carbon material shows good charge transfer kinetics and stable structural integrity.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/medium/am-2018-083809_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0001.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Cycle performance and (b) rate performance of the HC-1100 and HC-1100H samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b08380/asset/images/large/am-2018-083809_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b08380&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As expected, the K-doped hard carbon exhibits promising electrochemical performance, including high reversible capacity, stable cycle performance, and good rate performance. This was mainly ascribed to its specific structural properties. On the one hand, the expanded <i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub>-spacing originating from the superhigh potassium doping facilitates the Na<sup>+</sup> ionic diffusion and storage, leading to a fast and high capacity. On the other hand, the heteroatom-doped nature of hard carbon improves its electrical properties and also provides more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ionic adsorption and storage, thus improving the charge transfer kinetics. In addition, to more clearly illustrate the superior electrochemical performance of such carbon anode, <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">2</a> displays the comparisons with some previous report of biomass carbon anodes for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. Comparison of HC-1100 versus Different Biomass Carbons</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="left"><col align="left"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">materials</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">initial charge capacity (mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)/ICE</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">cyclability (mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HC-1100 (this work)</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">314 (50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)/69.2%</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">289 at 200th cycle (50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">activated peat moss carbon <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">133 (100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)/38.2%</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">122 at 100th cycle (100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">corncob carbon <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">152 (100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)/30.9%</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">139 at 100th cycle (100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we successfully synthesized a natural potassium-doped hard carbon as appropriate anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. We investigated its structural properties and found that the hard carbon possessed an expanded <i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub>-spacing originating from the superhigh potassium content. This specific structure facilitated the diffusion and storage of sodium ions into the bulk structure. The carbon anode thus exhibited promising electrochemical performance, including a high initial reversible capacity of 314 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a high capacity retention of 92.1% after 200 cycles. The fabrication is simple and environmental friendly with low cost, which make it possible to realize hard carbon for the large-scale application of SIBs. In addition, this work confirms the positive impact of the residual ions from the natural precursors, which provide insightful guidance on the exploration of biomass-derived carbon materials for energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="_suppInfo"></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b08380" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="authorInformationSection"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="authorInformationSection">Author Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><ul><li><span class="author-information-subsection-header">Corresponding Authors</span><ul><li><div class="authorItemInformation"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor strong">Ying Bai</span> - <span class="hlFld-Affiliation affiliation">School

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-ion batteries. A look into the future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Scrosati, Bruno; Hassoun, Jusef; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3287-3295</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  A crit. overview of the latest developments in the lithium ion batteries technol. is reported.  We first describe the evolution in the electrolyte area with particular attention to ionic liqs., discussing the expected application of these room temp. molten salts and listing the issues that still prevent their practical implementation.  The attention is then focused on the electrode materials presently considered the most promising for enhancing the energy d. of the batteries.  At the anode side a discussion is provided on the status of development of high capacity tin and silicon lithium alloys.  We show that the morphol. that is the most likely to ensure com. exploitation of these alloy electrodes is that involving carbon-based nanocomposites.  We finally touch on super-high-capacity batteries, discussing the key cases of lithium-sulfur and lithium-air and attempting to forecast their chances to eventually reach the status of practically appealing energy storage systems.  We conclude with a brief reflection on the amt. of lithium reserves in view of its large use in the case of global conversion from gasoline-powered cars to hybrid and elec. cars.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpRNpDW-RUP-rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljfOFHW0_5FGw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1Cqs7nN&amp;md5=074f36837a3cfe76f980bbd5ac5da973</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1126/science.1212741"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dunn, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kamath, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J. M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrical Energy Storage for the Grid: A Battery of Choices</span>. <i>Science</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>334</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">928</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">935</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
