            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Because of their abundance, Na and Ca can be attractive in ion batteries for large-scale grid storage.  However, many of the anode materials being pursued have limitations including vol. expansion, lack of passivating films, and slow kinetics.  Here, the authors study the adsorption of Na and Ca on graphene with di-vacancy and Stone-Wales defects in graphene.  Although adsorption of Na and Ca is not possible on pristine graphene, enhanced adsorption is obsd. on defective graphene because of increased charge transfer between the adatoms and defects.  The capacity of graphene increases with the d. of the defects.  For the max. possible di-vacancy defect densities, capacities of 1450 and 2900 mA-h/g for Na- and Ca-ion batteries, resp., can be achieved.  For Stone-Wales defects, max. capacities of 1071 and 2142 mA-h/g for Na and Ca, resp. were found.  The results provide guidelines to create better high-capacity anode materials for Na- and Ca-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwgqHKnhF6b7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh2sJ3pIAo6Qg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXnsVKhsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=b2f22102ae353e19690e42919f793611</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.5b05443"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fluorine-Doped Carbon Particles Derived from Lotus Petioles as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fluorine-Doped Carbon Particles Derived from Lotus Petioles as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Pengzi; Qiao, Bin; Du, Yichen; Li, Yafei; Zhou, Xiaosi; Dai, Zhihui; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">119</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In contrast to the extensive study of the electrochem. performance of conventional C materials in Na-ion batteries, there was scarcely any study of Na storage property of F-doped C.  Here the authors report for the 1st time the application of F-doped C particles (F-CP) synthesized through pyrolysis of lotus petioles as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  Electrochem. tests demonstrate that the F-CP electrode delivers an initial charge capacity of 230 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 between 0.001 and 2.8 V, which greatly outperforms the corresponding value of 149 mA h g-1 for the counterpart banana peels-derived C (BPC).  Even under 200 mA g-1, the F-CP electrode could still exhibit a charge capacity of 228 mA h g-1 with initial charge capacity retention of 99.1% after 200 cycles compared to the BPC electrode with 107 mA h g-1 and 71.8%.  The F-doping and the large interlayer distance as well as the disorder structure contribute to a lowering of the Na ion insertion-extn. barrier, thus promoting the Na+ diffusion and providing more active sites for Na+ storage.  In specific, the F-CP electrode shows longer low-discharge-plateau and better kinetics than does the common C-based electrode.  The unique electrochem. performance of F-CP enriches the existing knowledge of the C-based electrode materials and broadens avenues for rational design of anode materials in Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoQqQAaLqKEFLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh2sJ3pIAo6Qg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsVeqsb7I&amp;md5=8b9052e5c058808551c562c94d9a5a64</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA05451B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Amorphous Monodispersed Hard Carbon Micro-Spherules Derived from Biomass as a High Performance Negative Electrode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Amorphous monodispersed hard carbon micro-spherules derived from biomass as a high performance negative electrode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yunming; Xu, Shuyin; Wu, Xiaoyan; Yu, Juezhi; Wang, Yuesheng; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Li, Hong; Chen, Liquan; Huang, Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are expected to be a promising com. alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale and low-cost elec. energy storage applications in the near future.  Despite this, the absence of a suitable neg. electrode material hinders their development.  In this contribution, the authors synthesized monodispersed hard carbon spherules (HCS) from an abundant biomass of sucrose, and studied the influence of the carbonization temp. on the microstructure and electrochem. performance.  The initial coulombic efficiency of the HCS was increased to 83% by coating its surface with soft carbon through the pyrolysis of toluene.  The plateau capacity at the low potential region increased with increasing carbonization temp.  The HCS carbonized at 1600° showed the highest plateau capacity (220 mA-h/g) and excellent cycling performance with a capacity retention of 93% after 100 cycles.  When coupled with an air-stable P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 pos. electrode, the full cell exhibited a high initial coulombic efficiency of 76%, a mean operating voltage of 3.5 V and excellent cycling performance.                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-Surface-Area Hard Carbon Anode for Na-Ion Batteries via Graphene Oxide as a Dehydration Agent</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Bommier, Clement; Jian, Zelang; Li, Xin; Carter, Rich; Vail, Sean; Lu, Yuhao; Lee, Jong-Jan; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are emerging as one of the most promising energy storage technologies, particularly for grid-level applications.  Among anode candidate materials, hard carbon is very attractive due to its high capacity and low cost.  However, hard carbon anodes often suffer a low first-cycle coulombic efficiency and fast capacity fading.  In this study, we discover that doping graphene oxide into sucrose, the precursor for hard carbon, can effectively reduce the sp. surface area of hard carbon to as low as 5.4 m2/g.  We further show that such doping can effectively prevent foaming during caramelization of sucrose and extend the pyrolysis burnoff of sucrose caramel over a wider temp. range.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A simple one-step thermal pyrolysis route has been developed to prep. carbon membrane from a natural leaf.  The carbonized leaf membrane possesses anisotropic surfaces and internal hierarchical porosity, exhibiting a high specific capacity of 360 mAh/g and a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 74.8% as a binder-free, current-collector-free anode for rechargeable sodium ion batteries.                         cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lignin-based electrospun carbon nanofibrous webs as free-standing and binder-free electrodes for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Juan; Yu, Bao-jun; Shi, Zhi-qiang; Wang, Cheng-yang; Chong, Chuan-bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">272</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">800-807</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Low-cost and bio-based carbon nanofibrous webs (PL-CNFs) are fabricated from polyacrylonitrile (PAN)-refined lignin (RL) which is extd. from hardwood lignosulfonate via simple eletrospinning followed by stabilization and carbonization.  The effects of the PAN/RL mass ratios varying from 9/1,7/3 to 5/5 and heat-treatment temps. (HTTs) in the range from 800, 1000 to 1300 °C on morphol. and structure of PL-CNFs are systematically studied.  Due to unique morphol. and weakly ordered turbostratic microstructure of the 3-D conductive composite networks, the PL-CNFs anode obtained at 1300 °C with a mass ratio of 5/5 exhibits a high reversible capacity of 292.6 mA h g-1 with an initial efficiency of 70.5% at a const. c.d. of 0.02 A g-1 when used as free-standing and binder-free anodes for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  The anode also presents high rate capability (210 and 80 mA h g-1 at 0.4 and 1 A g-1, resp.) and excellent cycle stability (247 mA h g-1 reversible capacity with 90.2% capacity retention ratio at 0.1 A g-1 over 200 cycles).  It is demonstrated that biomass waste lignin can be applied as a promising precursor to fabricate low-cost-high performance carbon electrode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo6xoSPzjjd3bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgFCdtuHNqPkQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhsFCnsr3F&amp;md5=f017b8fdfb8a99b1a0c2b4e860724993</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1149/1.1394081"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahn, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An In Situ Small-Angle X-Ray Scattering Study of Sodium Insertion into a Nanoporous Carbon Anode Material within an Operating Electrochemical Cell</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An in situ small-angle X-ray scattering study of sodium insertion into a nanoporous carbon anode material within an operating electrochemical cell</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Stevens, D. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2000</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">147</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">An electrochem. cell with beryllium X-ray windows has been designed and used for in situ small-angle X-ray-scattering studies of operating electrodes for the first time.  This cell is ideally suited to the study of the filling of nanoscopic pores in solids by electrochem. transported atoms.  The mechanism of electrochem. lithium and sodium insertion in nanoporous carbonaceous materials has been the subject of some recent controversy, which is resolved by the studies reported here.  We show indisputable evidence that the filling of the pores accounts for all the sodium (and lithium) inserted into these carbons at a chem. potential near that of metallic sodium (or lithium).  At lower chem. potential (higher cell voltage), sodium (or lithium) is inserted between graphene layers in an intercalation mechanism.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNml8XpXIetrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgFCdtuHNqPkQ"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NMR study for electrochemically inserted Na in hard carbon electrode of sodium ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gotoh, Kazuma; Ishikawa, Toru; Shimadzu, Saori; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Komaba, Shinichi; Takeda, Kazuyuki; Goto, Atsushi; Deguchi, Kenzo; Ohki, Shinobu; Hashi, Kenjiro; Shimizu, Tadashi; Ishida, Hiroyuki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">225</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The state of Na inserted in the hard C electrode of a Na ion battery having practical cyclability was studied using solid state 23Na NMR.  The spectra of C samples charged (reduced) &gt; 50 mAh g-1 showed clear three components.  Two peaks at 9.9 ppm and 5.2 ppm were ascribed to reversible Na stored between disordered graphene sheets in hard C because the shift of the peaks was invariable with changing strength of external magnetic field.  One broad signal at ∼-9 to -16 ppm was assigned to Na in heterogeneously distributed closed nanopores in hard C.  Low temp. 23Na static and magic angle spinning NMR spectra didn't split or shift whereas the spectral pattern of 7Li NMR for Li-inserted hard C changes depending on the temp.  This strongly suggests that the exchange of Na atoms between different sites in hard C is slow.  Na doesn't form quasi-metallic clusters in closed nanopores of hard C although Na assembles at nanopores while the cell is electrochem. charged.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp0ou4lb71gFrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgFCdtuHNqPkQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhslKqs7bK&amp;md5=e1c310912b6d86be109fde24b729a97c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501588"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ghimbeu, C. E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Trash to Treasure: from Harmful Algal Blooms to High-Performance Electrodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Environ. Sci. Technol.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>49</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">12543</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">12550</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.est.5b03882</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D49%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D12543-12550%26journal%3DEnviron.%2BSci.%2BTechnol.%26author%3DX.%2BMeng%26author%3DP.%2BE.%2BSavage%26author%3DD.%2BDeng%26title%3DTrash%2Bto%2BTreasure%253A%2Bfrom%2BHarmful%2BAlgal%2BBlooms%2Bto%2BHigh-Performance%2BElectrodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.est.5b03882&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b08380&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.est.5b03882&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Trash to Treasure: From Harmful Algal Blooms to High-Performance Electrodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Meng, Xinghua; Savage, Phillip E.; Deng, Da</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Environmental Science &amp; Technology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12543-12550</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ESTHAG</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-936X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Harmful algal blooms (HABs) are frequently reported around the globe.  HABs are typically caused by the so-called blue-green algae in eutrophic waters.  These fast-growing HABs could be a good source for biomass.  Unlike terrestrial plants, they need no land or soil.  If HABs could be harvested on a large scale, it could not only possible to mitigate the issue of HABs but also provide a source of biomass.  Herein, we demonstrate a facile procedure for converting the HABs into a promising high-performance neg.-electrode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The carbon material derived from blue-green algae demonstrated promising electrochem. performance in reversible sodium storage.  The algae used in this work was collected directly from Lake Erie during the algal blooms that affected 500 000 residents in Toledo in 2014.  The carbon, derived from the freshly collected HABs by calcination in argon without any addnl. purifn. process, delivered a highly stable reversible specific capacity (∼230 mAh/g at a testing current of 20 mA/g) with nearly 100% Columbic efficiency in sodium storage.  Impressive rate performance was achieved with a capacity of ∼135 mAh/g even after the testing current was increased fivefold.  This proof of concept provides a promising route for mitigating the issue of HABs as "trash" and for generating high-capacity, low-cost electrodes for SIBs as "treasure".</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNEt5qa3wZzLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgFCdtuHNqPkQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsFejt7zO&amp;md5=2317e7d9688a6d099ca89ede2ebcb15b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1016/j.apsusc.2016.03.204"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Biomass Carbon Micro/Nano-Structures Derived from Ramie Fibers and Corncobs As Anode Materials for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Appl. Surf. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>379</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">73</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">82</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.apsusc.2016.03.204</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D379%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D73-82%26journal%3DAppl.%2BSurf.%2BSci.%26author%3DQ.%2BJiang%26author%3DZ.%2BZhang%26author%3DS.%2BYin%26author%3DZ.%2BGuo%26author%3DS.%2BWang%26author%3DC.%2BFeng%26title%3DBiomass%2BCarbon%2BMicro%252FNano-Structures%2BDerived%2Bfrom%2BRamie%2BFibers%2Band%2BCorncobs%2BAs%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BLithium-Ion%2Band%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.apsusc.2016.03.204&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b08380&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.apsusc.2016.03.204&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Biomass carbon micro/nano-structures derived from ramie fibers and corncobs as anode materials for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang, Qiang; Zhang, Zhenghao; Yin, Shengyu; Guo, Zaiping; Wang, Shiquan; Feng, Chuanqi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">379</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">73-82</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ASUSEE</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0169-4332</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Three-dimensional (3D) rod-like carbon micro-structures derived from natural ramie fibers and two-dimensional (2D) carbon nanosheets derived from corncobs have been fabricated by heat treatment at 700 °C under argon atomsphere.  The structure and morphol. of the as-obtained ramie fiber carbon (RFC) and corncob carbon (CC) were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD), Raman spectroscopy, SEM (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) technique.  The electrochem. performances of the biomass carbon-based anode in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) were investigated.  When tested as anode material for lithium ion batteries, both the RFC microrods and CC nanosheets exhibited high capacity, excellent rate capability, and stable cyclability.  The specific capacity were still as high as 489 and 606 mAhg-1 after 180 cycles when cycled at room temp. in a 3.0-0.01 V potential (vs. Li/Li+) window at c.d. of 100 mAg-1, resp., which are much higher than that of graphite (375 mAhg-1) under the same c.d.  Although the anodes in sodium ion batteries showed poorer specific capability than that in lithium-ion batteries, they still achieve a reversible sodium intercalation capacity of 122 and 139 mAhg-1 with similar cycling stability.  The feature of stable cycling performance makes the biomass carbon derived from natural ramie fibers and corncobs to be promising candidates as electrodes in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries and lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGolw96bm3lfH7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liUv9Cgq7EvSA"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  A crit. overview of the latest developments in the lithium ion batteries technol. is reported.  We first describe the evolution in the electrolyte area with particular attention to ionic liqs., discussing the expected application of these room temp. molten salts and listing the issues that still prevent their practical implementation.  The attention is then focused on the electrode materials presently considered the most promising for enhancing the energy d. of the batteries.  At the anode side a discussion is provided on the status of development of high capacity tin and silicon lithium alloys.  We show that the morphol. that is the most likely to ensure com. exploitation of these alloy electrodes is that involving carbon-based nanocomposites.  We finally touch on super-high-capacity batteries, discussing the key cases of lithium-sulfur and lithium-air and attempting to forecast their chances to eventually reach the status of practically appealing energy storage systems.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources. B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, K. S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Li-Ion Rechargeable Battery: A Perspective</span>. <i>J. Am. Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Li-Ion Rechargeable Battery: A Perspective</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goodenough, John B.; Park, Kyu-Sung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">135</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1167-1176</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Each cell of a battery stores elec. energy as chem. energy in two electrodes, a reductant (anode) and an oxidant (cathode), sepd. by an electrolyte that transfers the ionic component of the chem. reaction inside the cell and forces the electronic component outside the battery.  The output on discharge is an external electronic current I at a voltage V for a time Δt.  The chem. reaction of a rechargeable battery must be reversible on the application of a charging I and V.  Crit. parameters of a rechargeable battery are safety, d. of energy that can be stored at a specific power input and retrieved at a specific power output, cycle and shelf life, storage efficiency, and cost of fabrication.  Conventional ambient-temp. rechargeable batteries have solid electrodes and a liq. electrolyte.  The pos. electrode (cathode) consists of a host framework into which the mobile (working) cation is inserted reversibly over a finite solid-soln. range.  The solid-soln. range, which is reduced at higher current by the rate of transfer of the working ion across electrode/electrolyte interfaces and within a host, limits the amt. of charge per electrode formula unit that can be transferred over the time Δt = Δt(I).  Moreover, the difference between energies of the LUMO and the HOMO of the electrolyte, i.e., electrolyte window, dets. the max. voltage for a long shelf and cycle life.  The max. stable voltage with an aq. electrolyte is 1.5 V; the Li-ion rechargeable battery uses an org. electrolyte with a larger window, which increase the d. of stored energy for a given Δt.  Anode or cathode electrochem. potentials outside the electrolyte window can increase V, but they require formation of a passivating surface layer that must be permeable to Li+ and capable of adapting rapidly to the changing electrode surface area as the electrode changes vol. during cycling.  A passivating surface layer adds to the impedance of the Li+ transfer across the electrode/electrolyte interface and lowers the cycle life of a battery cell.  Moreover, formation of a passivation layer on the anode robs Li from the cathode irreversibly on an initial charge, further lowering the reversible Δt.  These problems plus the cost of quality control of manufg. plague development of Li-ion rechargeable batteries that can compete with the internal combustion engine for powering elec. cars and that can provide the needed low-cost storage of elec. energy generated by renewable wind and/or solar energy.  Chemists are contributing to incremental improvements of the conventional strategy by investigating and controlling electrode passivation layers, improving the rate of Li+ transfer across electrode/electrolyte interfaces, identifying electrolytes with larger windows while retaining a Li+ cond. σLi &gt; 10-3 S cm-1, synthesizing electrode morphologies that reduce the size of the active particles while pinning them on current collectors of large surface area accessible by the electrolyte, lowering the cost of cell fabrication, designing displacement-reaction anodes of higher capacity that allow a safe, fast charge, and designing alternative cathode hosts.  However, new strategies are needed for batteries that go beyond powering hand-held devices, such as using electrode hosts with two-electron redox centers; replacing the cathode hosts by materials that undergo displacement reactions (e.g. sulfur) by liq. cathodes that may contain flow-through redox mols., or by catalysts for air cathodes; and developing a Li+ solid electrolyte separator membrane that allows an org. and aq. liq. electrolyte on the anode and cathode sides, resp.  Opportunities exist for the chemist to bring together oxide and polymer or graphene chem. in imaginative morphologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqqKg_SkQGkZrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liiRLu0YIBl6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXktFSisw%253D%253D&amp;md5=9b3d655905f4b3dc188e98f8370452df</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1038/nchem.1376"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, H.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An improved high-performance lithium-air battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jung, Hun-Gi; Hassoun, Jusef; Park, Jin-Bum; Sun, Yang-Kook; Scrosati, Bruno</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Although dominating the consumer electronics markets as the power source of choice for popular portable devices, the common lithium battery is not yet suited for use in sustainable electrified road transport.  The development of advanced, higher-energy lithium batteries is essential in the rapid establishment of the elec. car market.  Owing to its exceptionally high energy potentiality, the lithium-air battery is a very appealing candidate for fulfilling this role.  However, the performance of such batteries is limited to only a few charge-discharge cycles with low rate capability.  Here, by choosing a suitable stable electrolyte and appropriate cell design, a lithium-air battery capable of operating over many cycles with capacity and rate values as high as 5,000 mAh g-1carbon and 3 A g-1carbon, resp. is demonstrated.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpqNKJTie6WmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liiRLu0YIBl6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOisrzJ&amp;md5=2e02460553edc21e1ea8b73982ed63bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40811f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, S.                 10.1039/c3ee40811f</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Sung You; Kim, Youngjin; Park, Yuwon; Choi, Aram; Choi, Nam-Soon; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2067-2081</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHBS0wIkomorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liiRLu0YIBl6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVWmtL%252FE&amp;md5=023136cc0e5ed94e18ce0d526a0000a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liiRLu0YIBl6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Slater, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liiRLu0YIBl6A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06467D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rapid consumption of nonrenewable resources resulted in an ever-increasing problem of CO2 emissions that has motivated people for studying the harvesting of energy from renewable alternatives (e.g. solar and wind).  Efficient electrochem. energy storage devices play a crucial role in storing harvested energies in the daily lives.  For example, rechargeable batteries can store energy generated by solar cells during the daytime and release it during night-time.  In particular, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have received considerable attention ever since their early commercialization in 1990s.  However, with initiatives by several governments to build large-scale energy grids to store energy for cities, problems such as the high cost and limited availability of Li starts to become major issues.  Na, which also belongs to Group 1 of the periodic table, has comparable electrochem. properties to Li, and more importantly it is considerably more accessible than Li.  Nonetheless, research into Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is currently still in its infancy compared to LIBs, although great leaps and bounds have been made recently in terms of research and development into this technol.  Here in this review, the authors summarize the recent advancements made, also covering the prospective materials for both the battery cathode and anode.  Addnl., opinions on possible solns. through correlating trends in recent papers will be suggested.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqaYAOgglkaCrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhC278xRWjc6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXjs1yhsbo%253D&amp;md5=95489f575e9ded7e312db8576036b208</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1002/wene.136"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nithya, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gopukumar, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium Ion Batteries: A Newer Electrochemical Storage</span>. <i>Wiley Interdiscip.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Vehicle electrification is one of the most significant solns. that address the challenges of fossil fuel depletion, global warming, CO2 pollution, and so on.  To mitigate these issues, recent research mainly focuses on finding clean energy storage devices such as batteries, supercapacitors, fuel cells, and so forth.  Owing to the outstanding energy and power d., lithium-ion batteries (LIB) have captured the market for portable electronics, hybrid elec. vehicles, plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles, and so on.  During 1970-1980s, electrode materials for both LIBs and sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) were investigated but higher energy and power d. of LIBs have made it a popular candidate for portable electronics.  Issues arise on the availability of lithium reserves, so it is high time we take a look at finding alternative energy storage system without compromising on the energy and power d. of the state-of-the-art LIBs.  Therefore, researchers have revisited NIBs and recent developments have contributed towards discovering new electrode materials to match the energy and power d. of LIBs at low cost.  While a variety of pos. and neg. electrode materials have been investigated for NIBs so far, the influence of voltage, capacity, cycle life, and vol. expansion of neg. electrodes on Na+ ion extn. and insertion are more as compared with LIBs. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1600275</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/advs.201600275</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-Type Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ni Qiao; Bai Ying; Wu Feng; Wu Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries, representative members of the post-lithium-battery club, are very attractive and promising for large-scale energy storage applications.  The increasing technological improvements in sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries) are being driven by the demand for Na-based electrode materials that are resource-abundant, cost-effective, and long lasting.  Polyanion-type compounds are among the most promising electrode materials for Na-ion batteries due to their stability, safety, and suitable operating voltages.  The most representative polyanion-type electrode materials are Na3V2(PO4)3 and NaTi2(PO4)3 for Na-based cathode and anode materials, respectively.  Both show superior electrochemical properties and attractive prospects in terms of their development and application in Na-ion batteries.  Carbonophosphate Na3MnCO3PO4 and amorphous FePO4 have also recently emerged and are contributing to further developing the research scope of polyanion-type Na-ion batteries.  However, the typical low conductivity and relatively low capacity performance of such materials still restrict their development.  This paper presents a brief review of the research progress of polyanion-type electrode materials for Na-ion batteries, summarizing recent accomplishments, highlighting emerging strategies, and discussing the remaining challenges of such systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRzOrk04pyNfsjl5L8hwNX0fW6udTcc2ebPyl_ZUgjX9bntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1cvgtVOitA%253D%253D&amp;md5=f4bc597b44df5e4e4c0b0a698c9a4d56</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201704880"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Self-Supported 3D Array Electrodes for Sodium Microbatteries</span>. <i>Adv.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Peat moss, a wild plant that covers 3% of the earth's surface, serves as an ideal precursor to create Na ion battery (NIB) anodes with some of the most attractive electrochem. properties ever reported for carbonaceous materials.  By inheriting the unique cellular structure of peat moss leaves, the resultant materials are composed of 3-dimensional macroporous interconnected networks of C nanosheets (as thin as 60 nm).  The peat moss tissue is highly cross-linked, being rich in lignin and hemicellulose, suppressing the nucleation of equil. graphite even at 1100°.  Rather, the carbons form highly ordered pseudographitic arrays with substantially larger intergraphene spacing (0.388 nm) than graphite (c/2 = 0.3354 nm).  XRD anal. demonstrates that this allows for significant Na intercalation to occur even &lt;0.2 V vs. Na/Na+.  By also incorporating a mild (300°) air activation step, the authors introduce hierarchical micro- and mesoporosity that tremendously improves the high rate performance through facile electrolyte access and further reduced Na ion diffusion distances.  The optimized structures (carbonization at 1100° + activation) result in a stable cycling capacity of 298 mA-h g-1 (after 10 cycles, 50 mA g-1), with ∼150 mA-h g-1 of charge accumulating between 0.1 and 0.001 V with negligible voltage hysteresis in that region, nearly 100% cycling Coulombic efficiency, and superb cycling retention and high rate capacity (255 mA-h g-1 at the 210th cycle, stable capacity of 203 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq9g5dheVSGj7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liKJ-b2Fj-gxQ"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">A highly reversible, resource-abundant and low-cost anode is indispensable to the future success of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) in large-scale energy storage application.  The authors report the facile synthesis of a biomass-derived hard carbon for SIBs by 1-step pyrolysis of shaddock peel under an inert atm. without activation or any further treatments.  The pyrolytic carbon shows very high reversible sodium storage capacities up to 430.5 mA h/g at a c.d. of 30 mA/g and superior cycling stability with only 2.5% capacity loss over 200 charge-discharge cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Two types of hard carbon materials were synthesized by direct pyrolysis of com. polyvinyl chloride (PVC) particles and pyrolysis of PVC nanofibers at 600-800°, resp., where the nanofibers were prepd. by an electrospinning PVC precursors method.  These as-prepd. hard carbon samples were used as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  The hard carbon obtained from PVC nanofibers had a high reversible capacity of 271 mA-h/g and an initial coulombic efficiency of 69.9%, which were much superior to the one from com. PVC, namely, a reversible capacity of 206 mA-h/g and an initial coulombic efficiency of 60.9%.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Pursuing low-cost and high-performance anode materials is of great importance for developing practical sodium ion batteries.  In this work, mille-feuille shaped hard carbons derived from low-cost and environmentally friendly polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) nanofibres are fabricated via simple electrostatic spinning and followed by further pyrolysis at 800-1200 °C, as anode materials for sodium ion batteries.  The optimized sample HC-1000 carbonized at 1000 °C shows better particle size and low surface area, and achieves a good reversible capacity of 271 mA h g-1 with 94% capacity retention ratio over 100 cycles.  In addn., HC-1000 exhibits satisfactory rate performance, namely, the discharge capacities are 304, 264, 209, 142, 109 and 70 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 20, 40, 100, 200, 500 and 1000 mA g-1 after 10 cycles resp.  Even continuing with an addnl. 280 cycles at 200 mA g-1, the capacity retains 285 mA h g-1 when the current recovers to 20 mA g-1.  The mille-feuille shaped morphol., uniform particle size distribution and low surface area enable excellent electrochem. performances of PVP based hard carbon, which is expected to be a promising anode material for Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqsc4fA9VaAMrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liKJ-b2Fj-gxQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVCqsrw%253D&amp;md5=5d1f7ede56c8a28483fa69aed85eb0ed</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1021/jz2012066"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Slater, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are an attractive alternative to lithium-ion batteries that alleviate problems with lithium availability and cost.  Despite several studies of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries involving layered oxide materials, there are few low-voltage metal oxide anodes capable of operating sodium ion reversibly at room temp.  We have synthesized amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube electrodes directly grown on current collectors without binders and additives to use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  We find that only amorphous large diam. nanotubes (&gt;80 nm I.D.) can support electrochem. cycling with sodium ions.  These electrodes maximize their capacity in operando and reach reversible capacity of 150 mA-h/g in 15 cycles.  We also demonstrate for the first time a full cell all-oxide sodium-ion battery using amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube anode coupled to a Na1.0Li0.2Ni0.25Mn0.75Oδ cathode at room temp. exhibiting good rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr8q2bDG7YJCbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liKJ-b2Fj-gxQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1ChtLbP&amp;md5=66a2a71991a01d6823c9c01024f65c74</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201300139"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered sodium titanium oxide, Na2Ti3O7, is synthesized by a solid-state reaction method as a potential anode for sodium-ion batteries.  Through optimization of the electrolyte and binder, the microsized Na2Ti3O7 electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 188 mA h g-1 in 1 M NaFSI/PC electrolyte at a current rate of 0.1C in a voltage range of 0.0-3.0 V, with sodium alginate as binder.  The av. Na storage voltage plateau is found at ca. 0.3 V vs. Na+/Na, in good agreement with a first-principles prediction of 0.35 V. The Na storage properties in Na2Ti3O7 are investigated from thermodn. and kinetic aspects.  By reducing particle size, the nanosized Na2Ti3O7 exhibits much higher capacity, but still with unsatisfied cyclic properties.  The solid-state interphase layer on Na2Ti3O7 electrode is analyzed.  A zero-current overpotential related to thermodn. factors is obsd. for both nano- and microsized Na2Ti3O7.  The electronic structure, Na+ ion transport and cond. are investigated by the combination of first-principles calcn. and electrochem. characterizations.  On the basis of the vacancy-hopping mechanism, a quasi-3D energy favorable trajectory is proposed for Na2Ti3O7.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. performance of mesoporous carbon (C)/tin (Sn) anodes in Na-ion and Li-ion batteries is systematically investigated.  The mesoporous C/Sn anodes in a Na-ion battery shows similar cycling stability but lower capacity and poorer rate capability than that in a Li-ion battery.  The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.  Understanding of the reaction mechanism between Sn and Na ions will provide insight towards exploring and designing new alloy-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqL8pwZXJa53LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liwOuxfKz2tyQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsFKhtr8%253D&amp;md5=7fba4efb710816f1ca877f0494169a46</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201301584"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries are currently emerging as a replacement for lithium ion batteries due to sodium abundance in the earth's crust.  The recent development of sodium ion batteries anode has been achieved by the use of selected carbon as an anode.  The performance of the selected carbon materials such as the charge/discharge capacity, voltage profile and cyclic stability, depend strongly on the sizes, additives, electrolytes and structures.  Based on the contribution of the carbon-based materials as a replacement of the sodium ion batteries anode, much improvement has been achieved.  However, more investigations are still required in order to identify the suitable carbon materials that can provide and improve the anode properties.  In the same vein, alloy materials have gained increasing effort as high capacity anode and their combination with carbon have contributed to the development of sodium ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A porous hard carbon material was synthesized by the simple pyrolysis of H3PO4-treated biomass, i.e., pomelo peels, at 700 °C in N2.  The as-obtained hard carbon had a 3D connected porous structure and a large sp. surface area of 1272 m2 g-1.  XPS anal. showed that the carbon material was functionalized by O-contg. and P-contg. groups.  The porous hard carbon was used as an anode for sodium ion batteries and exhibited good cycling stability and rate capability, delivering a capacity of 181 mA h g-1 at 200 mA g-1 after 220 cycles and retaining a capacity of 71 mA h g-1 at 5 A g-1.  The sodium storage mechanisms of the porous hard carbon can be explained by Na+ intercalation into the disordered graphene layers, redox reaction of the surface O-contg. functional groups and Na+ storage in the nanoscale pores.  However, the porous hard carbon demonstrated a low coulombic efficiency of 27%, resulting from the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase film and the side reactions of surface phosphorus groups.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoldD5kAMcszLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liwOuxfKz2tyQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtVKmtbrO&amp;md5=9b28ca12b1ea3a55e7e2ada7ae1e05f9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1021/nn502045y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lotfabad, E.                         rightTab-cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Density Sodium and Lithium Ion Battery Anodes from Banana Peels</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Ding, Jia; Cui, Kai; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Kalisvaart, W. Peter; Hazelton, Michael; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Banana peel pseudographite (BPPG) offers superb dual functionality for Na ion battery (NIB) and Li ion battery (LIB) anodes.  The materials possess low surface areas (19-217 m2 g-1) and a relatively high electrode packing d. (0.75 g cm-3vs. ∼1 g cm-3 for graphite).  Tested against Na, BPPG delivers a gravimetric (and volumetric) capacity of 355 mA-h g-1 (by active material ∼700 mA-h cm-3, by electrode vol. ∼270 mA-h cm-3) after 10 cycles at 50 mA g-1.  A nearly flat ∼200 mA-h g-1 plateau that is &lt;0.1 V and a minimal charge/discharge voltage hysteresis make BPPG a direct electrochem. analog to graphite but with Na.  A charge capacity of 221 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 is degraded by 7% after 600 cycles, while a capacity of 336 mA-h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 is degraded by 11% after 300 cycles, in both cases with ∼100% cycling Coulombic efficiency.  For LIB applications BPPG offers a gravimetric (volumetric) capacity of 1090 mA-h g-1 (by material ∼2200 mA-h cm-3, by electrode ∼900 mA-h cm-3) at 50 mA g-1.  The reason that BPPG works so well for both NIBs and LIBs is that it uniquely contains 3 essential features: (a) dilated intergraphene spacing for Na intercalation at low voltages; (b) highly accessible near-surface nanopores for Li metal filling at low voltages; and (c) substantial defect content in the graphene planes for Li adsorption at higher voltages.  The &lt;0.1 V charge storage mechanism is fundamentally different for Na vs. for Li.  A combination of XRD and XPS demonstrates highly reversible Na intercalation rather than metal underpotential deposition.  By contrast, the same anal. proves metallic Li in the pores, with intercalation being much less pronounced.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvndZF6mFnK7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljre5qiv1VrXA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXpt1Krt70%253D&amp;md5=1ad531caaafd48d9545b9c814029eada</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.10.034"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Henderson, W.                         rightTab-cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Hard carbon nanoparticles as high-capacity, high-stability anodic materials for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Lifen; Cao, Yuliang; Henderson, Wesley A.; Sushko, Maria L.; Shao, Yuyan; Xiao, Jie; Wang, Wei; Engelhard, Mark H.; Nie, Zimin; Liu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon nanoparticles (HCNP) were synthesized by the pyrolysis of a polyaniline precursor.  The measured Na+ cation diffusion coeff. (10-13-10-15 cm2 s-1) in the HCNP obtained at 1150 °C is two orders of magnitude lower than that of Li+ in graphite (10-10-10-13 cm2 s-1), indicating that reducing the carbon particle size is very important for improving electrochem. performance.  These measurements also enable a clear visualization of the stepwise reaction phases and rate changes which occur throughout the insertion/extn. processes in HCNP, The electrochem. measurements also show that the nano-sized HCNP obtained at 1150 °C exhibited higher practical capacity at voltages lower than 1.2 V (vs. Na/Na+), as well as a prolonged cycling stability, which is attributed to an optimum spacing of 0.366 nm between the graphitic layers and the nano particular size resulting in a low-barrier Na+ cation insertion.  These results suggest that HCNP is a very promising high-capacity/stability anode for low cost sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo0215ZE-6YJbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljre5qiv1VrXA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVaqtrrE&amp;md5=93cb764059ea5aaa3841222611c03416</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref35" class="rightTabRefNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit35" data-doi="10.1021/nn502045y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lotfabad, E.                         rightTab-cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Density Sodium and Lithium Ion Battery Anodes from Banana Peels</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lotfabad, Elmira Memarzadeh; Ding, Jia; Cui, Kai; Kohandehghan, Alireza; Kalisvaart, W. Peter; Hazelton, Michael; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7115-7129</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Banana peel pseudographite (BPPG) offers superb dual functionality for Na ion battery (NIB) and Li ion battery (LIB) anodes.  The materials possess low surface areas (19-217 m2 g-1) and a relatively high electrode packing d. (0.75 g cm-3vs. ∼1 g cm-3 for graphite).  Tested against Na, BPPG delivers a gravimetric (and volumetric) capacity of 355 mA-h g-1 (by active material ∼700 mA-h cm-3, by electrode vol. ∼270 mA-h cm-3) after 10 cycles at 50 mA g-1.  A nearly flat ∼200 mA-h g-1 plateau that is &lt;0.1 V and a minimal charge/discharge voltage hysteresis make BPPG a direct electrochem. analog to graphite but with Na.  A charge capacity of 221 mA-h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 is degraded by 7% after 600 cycles, while a capacity of 336 mA-h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 is degraded by 11% after 300 cycles, in both cases with ∼100% cycling Coulombic efficiency.  For LIB applications BPPG offers a gravimetric (volumetric) capacity of 1090 mA-h g-1 (by material ∼2200 mA-h cm-3, by electrode ∼900 mA-h cm-3) at 50 mA g-1.  The reason that BPPG works so well for both NIBs and LIBs is that it uniquely contains 3 essential features: (a) dilated intergraphene spacing for Na intercalation at low voltages; (b) highly accessible near-surface nanopores for Li metal filling at low voltages; and (c) substantial defect content in the graphene planes for Li adsorption at higher voltages.  The &lt;0.1 V charge storage mechanism is fundamentally different for Na vs. for Li.  A combination of XRD and XPS demonstrates highly reversible Na intercalation rather than metal underpotential deposition.  By contrast, the same anal. proves metallic Li in the pores, with intercalation being much less pronounced.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvndZF6mFnK7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljre5qiv1VrXA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXpt1Krt70%253D&amp;md5=1ad531caaafd48d9545b9c814029eada</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref36" class="rightTabRefNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit36" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA04877C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Waste Biomass Derived Hard Carbon as a High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The utilization of renewable energies has become increasingly urgent for the sustainable development of our society.  Energy storage systems are essential in order to efficiently use these energies.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) show bright prospect in the application for energy storage markets due to the potential low cost originating from unlimited sources and wide distribution of Na.  However, the anode remains a great challenge in the industrialization of SIBs.  Hard carbon holds the most promising future among all reported anodes; however, there are still two main shortcomings such as high cost and low initial coulombic efficiency, which limit its application.  Here, we report a hard carbon material derived from an abundant and abandoned biomass of corn cobs (HCC) using a simple carbonization method.  The HCC shows excellent sodium storage performance with a reversible capacity of ca. 300 mA h g-1, a high initial coulombic efficiency of 86% and good cycling stability.  A prototype sodium-ion battery was prepd. to prove the application prospect using HCC1300 as the anode and Na0.9[Cu0.22Fe0.30Mn0.48]O2 as the cathode, exhibiting a high energy d. of 207 W h kg-1 and a long cycle life.  These excellent properties demonstrate that HCC is a potential candidate as an anode material for sodium-ion battery application.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrzP3444VBrIbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljre5qiv1VrXA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28Xht1eju7nN&amp;md5=f6776d35bde5f3fb873f88fe495141b6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref37" class="rightTabRefNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit37" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Murata, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishikawa, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ozeki, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakayama, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogata, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gotoh, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujiwara, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.
