            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To transform lithium ion batteries into large-scale energy storage technologies, high energy/power densities and long cycling life of carbon-based anodes must be achieved.  This requires revolutionary design of the anode's architecture that can facilitate fast electronic and ionic transport, and accommodate the electrode structural instability.  Here a thin-film electrode design is reported and its use is demonstrated in flexible, and large-area carbon-based anode assemblies.  The fabrication of electrodes is realized by sputtering a graphite target in the high-purity nitrogen atm., then high defect nitrogen-doped carbon nanofibers are deposited vertically onto copper substrates with a thin film configuration.  The high-defect nitrogen-doping enhances the lithium storage and transport, the orientation growth mechanism improves the charge transfer, and the compact configuration makes high tap d. possible.  As a result, the thin films exhibit a high specific capacity of ∼500 mA h g-1, namely a vol. capacity of ∼100 mA h cm-3.  They also exhibit stable cycle performance (400 mA h g-1 after 200 cycles) and good rate capability (450 mA h g-1 at 1 A g-1 rate).  This work opens up a new carbon-based anode design by using sputtering technol. for effectively incorporating high content nitrogen into carbon matrixes.  Such electrode architecture significantly improves the electrochem. performance of carbon-based materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqKjbw53cigibVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li5HrIsgTFL1w"> &gt;&gt;             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural degrdn. and low cond. of transition-metal oxides lead to severe capacity fading in lithium-ion batteries.  Recent efforts to solve this issue have mainly focused on using nanocomposites or hybrids by integrating nanosized metal oxides with conducting additives.  Here we design specific hierarchical structures and demonstrate their use in flexible, large-area anode assemblies.  Fabrication of these anodes is achieved via oxidative growth of copper oxide nanowires onto copper substrates followed by radio-frequency sputtering of carbon-nitride films, forming freestanding three-dimensional arrays with core-shell nano-architecture.  Cable-like copper oxide/carbon-nitride core-shell nanostructures accommodate the vol. change during lithiation-delithiation processes, the three-dimensional arrays provide abundant electroactive zones and electron/ion transport paths, and the monolithic sandwich-type configuration without addnl. binders or conductive agents improves energy/power densities of the whole electrode.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrqBdeStoQ88rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li5HrIsgTFL1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XpsF2qtr8%253D&amp;md5=a687c24831d3954604b574950a4d5b89</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, a predominantly chosen anode material for com. lithium ion batteries (LIBs), was reported to have negligible intercalation capacity as an anode for sodium ion batteries (NIBs).  Disordered carbon exhibits high Na intercalation capacity and emerges as a leading candidate for NIB applications.  However, the mechanism of Na+ ion insertion into disordered carbon is still controversial.  Here, the authors propose an ab initio model for disordered carbon and study the intercalation mechanism of Na into the layered domains.  The ab initio calcns. reveal that a larger interlayer distance and the presence of defects can effectively overcome the van der Waals interaction between graphene sheets and help Na intercalation to form NaC8.  The calcn. results clarify the mechanism of the Na intercalation and account for the presence of sloping and flat regions of charge-discharge curves in disordered carbon reported in numerous expts.  This reveals new prospects for helping Na intercalation into graphite.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp7ZZtoy-aAAbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li5HrIsgTFL1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXkvFGiu7o%253D&amp;md5=b597dc05c2bcabe07b0930d469e47dde</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1016/j.jelechem.2015.07.032"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chong, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Properties and Sodium Insertion Behavior of Phenolic Resin-Based Hard Carbon Microspheres Obtained by a Hydrothermal Method</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Because of their abundance, Na and Ca can be attractive in ion batteries for large-scale grid storage.  However, many of the anode materials being pursued have limitations including vol. expansion, lack of passivating films, and slow kinetics.  Here, the authors study the adsorption of Na and Ca on graphene with di-vacancy and Stone-Wales defects in graphene.  Although adsorption of Na and Ca is not possible on pristine graphene, enhanced adsorption is obsd. on defective graphene because of increased charge transfer between the adatoms and defects.  The capacity of graphene increases with the d. of the defects.  For the max. possible di-vacancy defect densities, capacities of 1450 and 2900 mA-h/g for Na- and Ca-ion batteries, resp., can be achieved.  For Stone-Wales defects, max. capacities of 1071 and 2142 mA-h/g for Na and Ca, resp. were found.  The results provide guidelines to create better high-capacity anode materials for Na- and Ca-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwgqHKnhF6b7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li5HrIsgTFL1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXnsVKhsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=b2f22102ae353e19690e42919f793611</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.5b05443"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fluorine-Doped Carbon Particles Derived from Lotus Petioles as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In contrast to the extensive study of the electrochem. performance of conventional C materials in Na-ion batteries, there was scarcely any study of Na storage property of F-doped C.  Here the authors report for the 1st time the application of F-doped C particles (F-CP) synthesized through pyrolysis of lotus petioles as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  Electrochem. tests demonstrate that the F-CP electrode delivers an initial charge capacity of 230 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 between 0.001 and 2.8 V, which greatly outperforms the corresponding value of 149 mA h g-1 for the counterpart banana peels-derived C (BPC). The F-doping and the large interlayer distance as well as the disorder structure contribute to a lowering of the Na ion insertion-extn. barrier, thus promoting the Na+ diffusion and providing more active sites for Na+ storage.  In specific, the F-CP electrode shows longer low-discharge-plateau and better kinetics than does the common C-based electrode.                         rightTab-cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-Surface-Area Hard Carbon Anode for Na-Ion Batteries via Graphene Oxide as a Dehydration Agent</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Luo, Wei; Bommier, Clement; Jian, Zelang; Li, Xin; Carter, Rich; Vail, Sean; Lu, Yuhao; Lee, Jong-Jan; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are emerging as one of the most promising energy storage technologies, particularly for grid-level applications.  Among anode candidate materials, hard carbon is very attractive due to its high capacity and low cost.  However, hard carbon anodes often suffer a low first-cycle coulombic efficiency and fast capacity fading.  In this study, we discover that doping graphene oxide into sucrose, the precursor for hard carbon, can effectively reduce the sp. surface area of hard carbon to as low as 5.4 m2/g.  We further show that such doping can effectively prevent foaming during caramelization of sucrose and extend the pyrolysis burnoff of sucrose caramel over a wider temp. range.  The obtained low-surface-area hard carbon greatly improves the first-cycle coulombic efficiency from 74% to 83% and delivers a very stable cycle life with 95% of capacity retention after 200 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrqaHV_SLIfobVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljB49cI7YKDrg"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Low-cost and bio-based carbon nanofibrous webs (PL-CNFs) are fabricated from polyacrylonitrile (PAN)-refined lignin (RL) which is extd. from hardwood lignosulfonate via simple eletrospinning followed by stabilization and carbonization.  The effects of the PAN/RL mass ratios varying from 9/1,7/3 to 5/5 and heat-treatment temps. (HTTs) in the range from 800, 1000 to 1300 °C on morphol. and structure of PL-CNFs are systematically studied.  Due to unique morphol. and weakly ordered turbostratic microstructure of the 3-D conductive composite networks, the PL-CNFs anode obtained at 1300 °C with a mass ratio of 5/5 exhibits a high reversible capacity of 292.6 mA h g-1 with an initial efficiency of 70.5% at a const. c.d. of 0.02 A g-1 when used as free-standing and binder-free anodes for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  The anode also presents high rate capability (210 and 80 mA h g-1 at 0.4 and 1 A g-1, resp.) and excellent cycle stability (247 mA h g-1 reversible capacity with 90.2% capacity retention ratio at 0.1 A g-1 over 200 cycles).         </div><div class="casAbstract">An electrochem. cell with beryllium X-ray windows has been designed and used for in situ small-angle X-ray-scattering studies of operating electrodes for the first time.  This cell is ideally suited to the study of the filling of nanoscopic pores in solids by electrochem. transported atoms.  The mechanism of electrochem. lithium and sodium insertion in nanoporous carbonaceous materials has been the subject of some recent controversy, which is resolved by the studies reported here.  We show indisputable evidence that the filling of the pores accounts for all the sodium (and lithium) inserted into these carbons at a chem. potential near that of metallic sodium (or lithium).  At lower chem. potential (higher cell voltage), sodium (or lithium) is inserted between graphene layers in an intercalation mechanism.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNml8XpXIetrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljB49cI7YKDrg"> &gt;&gt;             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The state of Na inserted in the hard C electrode of a Na ion battery having practical cyclability was studied using solid state 23Na NMR.  The spectra of C samples charged (reduced) &gt; 50 mAh g-1 showed clear three components.  Two peaks at 9.9 ppm and 5.2 ppm were ascribed to reversible Na stored between disordered graphene sheets in hard C because the shift of the peaks was invariable with changing strength of external magnetic field.  One broad signal at ∼-9 to -16 ppm was assigned to Na in heterogeneously distributed closed nanopores in hard C.  Low temp. 23Na static and magic angle spinning NMR spectra didn't split or shift whereas the spectral pattern of 7Li NMR for Li-inserted hard C changes depending on the temp.  This strongly suggests that the exchange of Na atoms between different sites in hard C is slow.  Na doesn't form quasi-metallic clusters in closed nanopores of hard C although Na assembles at nanopores while the cell is electrochem. charged.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp0ou4lb71gFrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljB49cI7YKDrg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhslKqs7bK&amp;md5=e1c310912b6d86be109fde24b729a97c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref52" class="rightTabRefNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit52" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501588"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ghimbeu, C.                 10.1021/acs.est.5b03882</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Trash to Treasure: From Harmful Algal Blooms to High-Performance Electrodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Meng, Xinghua; Savage, Phillip E.; Deng, Da</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Environmental Science &amp; Technology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Harmful algal blooms (HABs) are frequently reported around the globe.  HABs are typically caused by the so-called blue-green algae in eutrophic waters.  These fast-growing HABs could be a good source for biomass.  Unlike terrestrial plants, they need no land or soil.  If HABs could be harvested on a large scale, it could not only possible to mitigate the issue of HABs but also provide a source of biomass.  Herein, we demonstrate a facile procedure for converting the HABs into a promising high-performance neg.-electrode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The carbon material derived from blue-green algae demonstrated promising electrochem. performance in reversible sodium storage.  The algae used in this work was collected directly from Lake Erie during the algal blooms that affected 500 000 residents in Toledo in 2014.  The carbon, derived from the freshly collected HABs by calcination in argon without any addnl. purifn. process, delivered a highly stable reversible specific capacity (∼230 mAh/g at a testing current of 20 mA/g) with nearly 100% Columbic efficiency in sodium storage.  Impressive rate performance was achieved with a capacity of ∼135 mAh/g even after the testing current was increased fivefold.  This proof of concept provides a promising route for mitigating the issue of HABs as "trash" and for generating high-capacity, low-cost electrodes for SIBs as "treasure".</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNEt5qa3wZzLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljnmQtNpLZzTg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsFejt7zO&amp;md5=2317e7d9688a6d099ca89ede2ebcb15b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54" data-doi="10.1016/j.apsusc.2016.03.204"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Biomass Carbon Micro/Nano-Structures Derived from Ramie Fibers and Corncobs As Anode Materials for Lithium-Ion and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Appl. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>379</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">73</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">82</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.apsusc.2016.03.204</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Biomass carbon micro/nano-structures derived from ramie fibers and corncobs as anode materials for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang, Qiang; Zhang, Zhenghao; Yin, Shengyu; Guo, Zaiping; Wang, Shiquan; Feng, Chuanqi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">379</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">73-82</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ASUSEE</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0169-4332</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Three-dimensional (3D) rod-like carbon micro-structures derived from natural ramie fibers and two-dimensional (2D) carbon nanosheets derived from corncobs have been fabricated by heat treatment at 700 °C under argon atomsphere.  The structure and morphol. of the as-obtained ramie fiber carbon (RFC) and corncob carbon (CC) were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD), Raman spectroscopy, SEM (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) technique.  The electrochem. performances of the biomass carbon-based anode in lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) were investigated.  When tested as anode material for lithium ion batteries, both the RFC microrods and CC nanosheets exhibited high capacity, excellent rate capability, and stable cyclability.  The specific capacity were still as high as 489 and 606 mAhg-1 after 180 cycles when cycled at room temp. in a 3.0-0.01 V potential (vs. Li/Li+) window at c.d. of 100 mAg-1, resp., which are much higher than that of graphite (375 mAhg-1) under the same c.d.  Although the anodes in sodium ion batteries showed poorer specific capability than that in lithium-ion batteries, they still achieve a reversible sodium intercalation capacity of 122 and 139 mAhg-1 with similar cycling stability.  The feature of stable cycling performance makes the biomass carbon derived from natural ramie fibers and corncobs to be promising candidates as electrodes in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries and lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGolw96bm3lfH7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljnmQtNpLZzTg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XltVOjsrs%253D&amp;md5=13bd45fcfdc2a5c569b8e0e2a57e2fdc</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li></ul></div></div></div></div></article><div class="w-slide article__w-slide">

        <script nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

        <script nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC">
// LIT-455776 workaround
// Moving the popup before the header content for tab accessibility
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', () => {
    const headerNode = $('.header_content');
    const popupNode = $('.pairing-affiliations-popup');

    if (!headerNode.length || !popupNode.length)
        return;

    popupNode.insertBefore(headerNode);  
});

<script nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC">
"use strict"

	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {
        var check = false;

u|g560|gene|gf\-5|g\-mo|go(\.w|od)|gr(ad|un)|haie|hcit|hd\-(m|p|t)|hei\-|hi(pt|ta)|hp( i|ip)|hs\-c|ht(c(\-| |_|a|g|p|s|t)|tp)|hu(aw|tc)|i\-(20|go|ma)|i230|iac( |\-|\/)|ibro|idea|ig01|ikom|im1k|inno|ipaq|iris|ja(t|v)a|jbro|jemu|jigs|kddi|keji|kgt( |\/)|klon|kpt |kwc\-|kyo(c|k)|le(no|xi)|lg( g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90da8fd49efab325-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
